<div id="review_154578593" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/154578593" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3335664-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1551667298p2/3335664.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/154578593?book_show_action=true">Mar 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/3335664-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780788747601" href="/book/show/2079759.Parable_of_the_Sower">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">preacher's daughters, &quot;post-racial&quot; Americans, hyper-empaths</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=female-author">female-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=feminist">feminist</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=female-protagonist">female-protagonist</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=poc-author">poc-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=poc-protagonist">poc-protagonist</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=california">california</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=race-relations">race-relations</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer154578593&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer154578593" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9893530621536193528">Octavia Butler's vision of an American state on the brink of economic and social collapse seems all too near and plausible. Lauren Olamina, a young minister's daughter, lives in a gated community that falls prey to the violence and anarchy that's been eating away at the edges of civilization for years. It's a brutal novel, as everyone Lauren loves dies, and the deaths are often described in gruesome detail. Lauren herself suffers from a condition called hyper-empathy, which causes her to feel wh</span>
  <span id="freeText9893530621536193528" style="display:none">Octavia Butler's vision of an American state on the brink of economic and social collapse seems all too near and plausible. Lauren Olamina, a young minister's daughter, lives in a gated community that falls prey to the violence and anarchy that's been eating away at the edges of civilization for years. It's a brutal novel, as everyone Lauren loves dies, and the deaths are often described in gruesome detail. Lauren herself suffers from a condition called hyper-empathy, which causes her to feel what people around her feel... a very bad thing when people around her are being attacked, raped, and killed.<br /><br />But Lauren is anything but a fragile helpless character. She carries the seeds of a new religion, and she's been planning for the collapse since she was a child. This novel has a lot in common with similarly-themed sci-fi where the protagonist is a "chosen one" destined to lead his or her people out of chaos and barbarism, except that no one has "chosen" Lauren. She's decided for herself that this is something she has to do.<br /><br />The story takes us on Lauren's journey up the coast of California, as its highways are flooded with refugees and its cities burn (thanks in large part to a drug called "pyro" which turns people into psychotic pyromaniacs). What is most interesting is not Lauren's "adventures" (which are mostly just a series of tense encounters fraught with dangers, as she constantly has to weigh the need for allies against the hazards of trusting unknown people on the road) but the brutal laying bare of certain truths beneath our capitalist society. Lauren's father foresaw the coming collapse and tried to keep his family from being lured into company towns that were economic traps, but even he didn't foresee how bad things would really become. Lauren discovers that slavery is real, and has been real and common, right here in the U.S., for quite some time.<br /><br />You might think it's a cynical and overly pessimistic view of the future, but if you're not among the privileged classes, it's not far from how many people live today. Ultimately, the book gives some hope for the future, but there is certainly going to be more blood and tears along the way.<br /><br />This was actually my first time reading Octavia Butler, whom I really should read more of, and I'm definitely going to pick up the next book in the series.<br /><br /><b>ETA:</b> My review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/155186149" rel="nofollow noopener">Parable of the Talents</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9893530621536193528" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2661463074" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2661463074" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/155663-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581956036p2/155663.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2661463074?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/155663-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/155663-david?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/155663-david?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2661463074" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6546598312300612914">This is a riveting story about Lauren, a 15-year old girl in a dying world. Climate change has made the world warmer, and Lauren lives in a very dry California. She lives with her adopted family, before her neighborhood is destroyed by desperate people. It seems to be everybody for himself. This is a dystopia novel, where one small mistake can mean death by any number of ways--usually murder.<br/><br/>This is one of those books I just couldn't put down. It is so intense, and so engaging on different level</span>
  <span id="freeText6546598312300612914" style="display:none">This is a riveting story about Lauren, a 15-year old girl in a dying world. Climate change has made the world warmer, and Lauren lives in a very dry California. She lives with her adopted family, before her neighborhood is destroyed by desperate people. It seems to be everybody for himself. This is a dystopia novel, where one small mistake can mean death by any number of ways--usually murder.<br /><br />This is one of those books I just couldn't put down. It is so intense, and so engaging on different levels. Lauren is philosophical, and throughout the story she is surreptitiously developing her own religion. She doesn't really believe in the religion of her father, who is a preacher. Instead, she develops a naturalistic religion, <br /><br />The story is so plausible; what happens when a society breaks down? When laws mean very little? When police are no longer working for the greater good? When inflation has made money almost worthless? When you can trust nobody, because everybody is a stranger, looking out only for number one?<br /><br />Lauren is a courageous girl, leading a band of people who don't entirely share her vision of the future. But she has a handicap in this violent world; she is a "sharer", which means that she is ultra-empathic. She literally feels the pain of others. As a result, when she is attacked, if she strikes back she can become paralyzed with pain. And, this is certainly a "coming of age" story, where Lauren learns what she should communicate to other people, and what she should keep inside. Disaster can strike at any moment, without warning. Lauren's motto might be the same as the Boy Scouts' motto: <i>Be prepared</i>.<br /><br />Some people might call this novel "science fiction", but I certainly would not classify it as such. Other than the ultra-empath aspect, there is no science in it, and there is no advanced technology. Some people talk about launching a space ship to Mars, but that is crazy talk, given the state of society. I guess this might be classified as "speculative fiction". If you haven't read any of Octavia Butler's novels, I recommend giving it a try. They grab at me, and keep me reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6546598312300612914" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_57871741" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/57871741" />
    <a title="Parvoneh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2368492-parvoneh"><img alt="Parvoneh" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/57871741?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Parvoneh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Parvoneh" href="/user/show/2368492-parvoneh">Parvoneh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer57871741" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13333848070146764943">I just skimmed a few other Goodreads reviews of <i>Parable of the Sower</i> and felt confused about why difficult subject matter seems to be a weakness to many readers. If anything, I wish Octavia Butler were around so I could thank her for that. She wrote about survival, change, and power with incredible insight; she grapples with some Big Stuff but her novel, ideas, and genre also manage to be accessible. Butler's clarity is a strength and perhaps a stylistic weakness, but mostly I think there's some</span>
  <span id="freeText13333848070146764943" style="display:none">I just skimmed a few other Goodreads reviews of <i>Parable of the Sower</i> and felt confused about why difficult subject matter seems to be a weakness to many readers. If anything, I wish Octavia Butler were around so I could thank her for that. She wrote about survival, change, and power with incredible insight; she grapples with some Big Stuff but her novel, ideas, and genre also manage to be accessible. Butler's clarity is a strength and perhaps a stylistic weakness, but mostly I think there's something great about directly addressing change and survival--noting the potential horrors but also the possibilities of change, of creation, of our responsibility to ourselves and each other. Butler made that clear while maintaining the complexities of survival, and inspiring a lot of additional thought. On top of that, she demonstrated how sci fi can be relevant to race and gender issues, and how literature can be relevant, and enjoyable, for important work. I get so excited when I realize how remarkable that is, particularly while reading, and that happened often during <i>Parable of the Sower</i>. Right now I'm thinking of Octavia B as sort of a cross between Doris Lessing and Kazuo Ishiguro and Toni Morrison and oh, I just love her.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13333848070146764943" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1522180077" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1522180077" />
    <a title="Dannii Elle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44487975-dannii-elle"><img alt="Dannii Elle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508499419p2/44487975.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1522180077?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dannii Elle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dannii Elle" href="/user/show/44487975-dannii-elle">Dannii Elle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44487975-dannii-elle?shelf=science-fiction-sagacity">science-fiction-sagacity</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1522180077" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17530612488307042933">This is a really intense and interesting insight into a scarily believable future of our world. With water scarce and sold at an all-time premium and with the subsequent abolishment of the structured societal hierarchy as we know it, anarchy reigns on Earth.<br/><br/>Lauren lives a relatively safe life inside the confines of her walled community, on the outskirts of LA. Lauren's preacher father strove to create a community that thrived on the now almost-obsolete religious teachings he believes in. He exce</span>
  <span id="freeText17530612488307042933" style="display:none">This is a really intense and interesting insight into a scarily believable future of our world. With water scarce and sold at an all-time premium and with the subsequent abolishment of the structured societal hierarchy as we know it, anarchy reigns on Earth.<br /><br />Lauren lives a relatively safe life inside the confines of her walled community, on the outskirts of LA. Lauren's preacher father strove to create a community that thrived on the now almost-obsolete religious teachings he believes in. He excelled in his endeavour... until he didn't. Lauren then finds herself thrust into a new world she knows little about and must find her own way of viewing all it contains, without her father's guidance.<br /><br />I really liked how this managed to be religious without being overly preachy and the creation of Lauren's Earthseed beliefs was fascinating. This was both an insight to religion, from both a believer and a non-believer's perspective, as well as a look at how this way of thinking adapts to a dystopian world where little faith remains. Lauren is very much an active agent of change and her new way of thinking could just be humanity's salvation.<br /><br />Whilst these concepts put forward some interesting points of discussion, I did find the plot initially a little slow building. This was a fascinating insight to a recognisable yet ravaged world but the content of the plot did not progress much from this point, for quite some time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17530612488307042933" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1916154994" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14372514087488472274"> One of the best parts about being in a book club is that sometimes the picks would never be what you would have chosen for yourself. My Litsy Goes Postal group has so far brought two books to my radar that I never would have picked up if left to my own devices. I didn't LOVE either of them but at the same time, I loved I was challenged to reach beyond my reading comfort zone. <br/><br/>My Reactions:<br/><br/>-The reading flow was choppy for me initially. I really didn't get into the reading groove of this one unti</span>
  <span id="freeText14372514087488472274" style="display:none"> One of the best parts about being in a book club is that sometimes the picks would never be what you would have chosen for yourself. My Litsy Goes Postal group has so far brought two books to my radar that I never would have picked up if left to my own devices. I didn't LOVE either of them but at the same time, I loved I was challenged to reach beyond my reading comfort zone. <br /><br />My Reactions:<br /><br />-The reading flow was choppy for me initially. I really didn't get into the reading groove of this one until about chapter 6ish. <br />-Gosh, what a bleak apocalyptic view of humanity. I hope this never happens. <br />-The preachy, biblical ideas of Earthseed really didn't do much for me. The story of survival was the highlight of the book for me. <br />-Fascinating story that really was an original concept. Scarily so. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14372514087488472274" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('65qvd9/RhWbLs5jNmP/R4DOay+LcoMOR4ut/OhaNuVET5inykX1u3m3Xe8YSABxSAurdZLg+36q8dSbohtFsVw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('r2eHVrCvORo7Gxd7zJHZ1F1hFpu2ocITb8ZGHY9tMiVXGwHT/gPSop1/9HBGbhRmbBEAHdI/3igxWB/PHzHnIw==')})
  }

Davis has put the case for the latest abolition movement in American life: the abolition of the prison. As she quite correctly notes, American life is replete with abolition movements, and when they were engaged in these struggles, their chances of success seemed almost unthinkable. For generations of Americans, the abolition of slavery was sheerest illusion. Similarly,the entrenched system of racial segregation seemed to last forever, and generations lived in the midst of the practice, with few predicting its passage from custom. The brutal, exploitative (dare one say lucrative?) convict-lease system that succeeded formal slavery reaped millions to southern jurisdictions (and untold miseries for tens of thousands of men, and women). Few predicted its passing from the American penal landscape. Davis expertly argues how social movements transformed these social, political and cultural institutions, and made such practices untenable.In Are Prisons Obsolete?, Professor Davis seeks to illustrate that the time for the prison is approaching an end. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17959783597221236561">Octavia Estelle Butler was an American science fiction writer, one of the best-known among the few African-American women in the field. She won both Hugo and Nebula awards. In 1995, she became the first science fiction writer to receive the MacArthur Foundation "Genius" Grant.</span>

One of the world’s most respected authors of science fiction imagines an apocalyptic near-future Earth where a remarkable young woman discovers that her destiny calls her to try and change the world around her. Octavia E. Butler’s brilliant two-volume Earthseed saga offers a startling vision of an all-too-possible tomorrow, in which walls offer no protection from a civilization gone mad.  Parable of the Sower: In the aftermath of worldwide ecological and economic apocalypse, minister’s daughter Lauren Oya Olamina escapes the slaughter that claims the lives of her family and nearly every other member of their gated California community. Heading north with two young companions through an American wasteland, the courageous young woman faces dangers at every turn while spreading the word of a remarkable new religion that embraces survival and change.  Parable of the Talents: Called to the new, hard truth of Earthseed, the small community of the dispossessed that now surrounds Lauren Olamina looks to her—their leader—for guidance. But when the evil that has grown out of the ashes of human society destroys all she has built, the prophet is forced to choose between preserving her faith or her family.   The Earthseed novels cement Butler’s reputation as “one of the finest voices in fiction—period” (TheWashington Post Book World). Stunningly prescient and breathtakingly relevant to our times, this dark vision of a future America is a masterwork of powerful speculation that ushers us into a broken, dangerously divided world of bigotry, social inequality, mob violence, and ultimately hope.  <\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"13463161503608546922\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n
